13:I[27633,["619","static/chunks/619-f072ac750404f9da.js","909","static/chunks/909-df1df79f44c4b47c.js","499","static/chunks/app/chapters/%5Bchapter%5D/page-d164dc85b7b8428a.js"],"ChapterStudy"]
14:T53e,The biblical evidence points to papal Rome:
1. It comes from the fourth beast, which Daniel 7:23 identifies as the fourth kingdom and Daniel 2 has already shown to be Rome.
2. It appears among the ten horns, placing it in the territory of divided Western Rome rather than Asia, Africa, or the Greek period.
3. It rises after the ten horns are already present, so its decisive rise follows the breakup of Western Rome after A.D. 476.
4. It uproots three horns; the Heruli, Vandals, and Ostrogoths were removed as papal supremacy gained room to stand.
5. It becomes more stout than its fellows, growing from small beginnings into a power able to influence kings and nations.
6. It is diverse from the first horns, because it is religious-political rather than merely civil.
7. It has eyes like a man and a mouth speaking great things, pointing to human wisdom exalted above Scripture and blasphemous claims about divine authority.
8. It makes war on the saints and wears them out, matching the long history of persecution under coercive church-state power.
9. It thinks to change times and law, reaching toward the sphere of worship, conscience, and God's commandments.
10. It rules for a time, times, and half a time, the 1260-day prophetic period also expressed in Revelation as forty-two months or 1260 days and fulfilled from A.D. 538-1798.15:T53e,The biblical evidence points to papal Rome:

10. It rules for a time, times, and half a time, the 1260-day prophetic period also expressed in Revelation as forty-two months or 1260 days and fulfilled from A.D. 538-1798.18:T4a6,The verse gives the later actions and the time limit in numbered clarity:
1. It speaks great words against the Most High, which Scripture defines as blasphemy when a human claims divine status or authority to forgive sins.
2. It wears out the saints, so the issue is persecution of God's people, not merely disagreement over ideas.
3. It thinks to change times, reaching into what belongs to God, who alone rules times and seasons.
4. It thinks to change law, especially in relation to worship, obedience, and the commandments.
5. The saints are given into its hand, showing that God permits a limited period of oppression without surrendering final control.
6. The period is time, times, and half a time, or three and a half prophetic years.
7. Revelation restates the same period as forty-two months and 1260 days.
8. Numbers 14:34 and Ezekiel 4:6 give the biblical year-day principle, so the period represents 1260 years.
9. The dates A.D. 538-1798 fit the rise and historical wound of papal supremacy, ending with the papal captivity under Berthier.
10. Antiochus IV does not satisfy the Roman setting, the post-A.D. 476 timing, the law-changing claim, and the 1260-year scope together.19:T4a6,The verse gives the later actions and the time limit in numbered clarity:

The verse summarizes Medo-Persia's expansion with the force of a military map.","The directions fit Persian conquest. From its base in the east, Medo-Persia moved west against Babylon and Asia Minor, northward into regions around the Caspian and beyond, and southward toward Egypt. The empire became vast, organized, wealthy, and difficult to resist. In Daniel's sequence, it dominates from 539-331 B.C.","Yet the language of greatness is restrained. The ram becomes great, but it does not become final. Daniel 8 will later say the goat becomes very great and the little horn becomes exceeding great. The escalation prepares the reader to see that each power surpasses the former in reach or intensity, but each remains under judgment.","The text also says the ram did according to his will. That phrase becomes important in Daniel because self-will often marks rulers and systems that forget accountability. A kingdom may be used by God for a season and still need to answer for pride, violence, and self-exaltation.","Medo-Persia's greatness therefore should be read with both historical clarity and spiritual caution. Cyrus can be named by Isaiah as an instrument for God's purposes, yet the empire itself still belongs to the passing sequence of earthly kingdoms. God may work through rulers without making their kingdoms holy.","The phrase 'according to his will' is also spiritually searching. Empires often define greatness as the freedom to do whatever they desire. Scripture measures greatness differently. A power may have no earthly deliverer against it and still be answerable to the God who appoints its limits.","Medo-Persia reminds the conscience that strength is not sovereignty. A nation, institution, or person may push in every direction and meet little resistance, but unchecked success is not the same as divine approval. God sees the ram long before the goat arrives."],"explanation":"The ram pushes westward, northward, and southward. No beast can stand before it, and none can deliver from its hand. One reading sees it as paganism, the continual pagan opposition that is removed or transformed as papal Rome rises. The immediate prophetic flow gives that reading a slight contextual advantage: Daniel 8 moves from pagan Rome's horizontal conquest into a later religious-political phase, and history shows pagan imperial forms giving way to papal supremacy. The horn does not begin as a church power; it develops through Rome's transition.","A second reading sees the daily as Christ's continual heavenly ministry, obscured by counterfeit priesthood, sacrifice, mediation, confession, and worship. The vision moves from pagan Rome's military expansion to a later religious-political phase, so the daily can mark the pagan form that is taken away in the transition.\n2. Daniel 8 and Daniel 11 both show powers shifting through history rather than introducing an isolated sanctuary term without context.\n3. The rise of papal supremacy required the displacement, absorption, and transformation of pagan imperial Rome.\n4. The phrase works naturally in the chapter's movement from horizontal conquest to vertical religious claims.\n5. This reading explains why the same Roman horn can begin in pagan imperial power and later act through papal authority.","The case for Christ's continual ministry also deserves respect:\n1. The vision begins with Medo-Persia and Greece, moves through Rome, describes a little horn that becomes exceeding great, and reaches the time of the end. Antiochus IV may illustrate features of arrogance and temple desecration, but he does not exhaust the prophecy.","Several lines of evidence press the vision beyond Antiochus:\n1. Gabriel says the vision reaches the time of the end.\n2. The little horn becomes exceeding great, surpassing the scale of the preceding powers.\n3. The horn arises in the sequence after Greece and fits Rome's historical expansion.\n4. It attacks the Prince, not merely an earthly temple administration.\n5. It casts truth to the ground in a way that reaches worship and mediation.\n6. It is finally broken without hand, language that points to divine judgment rather than ordinary military reversal.\n7. Daniel 9 is needed to explain the time element, which reaches far beyond a short temple crisis.","The time-of-the-end language also connects Daniel 8 with Daniel 12, where sealed prophecy is understood more fully near the end. The vision is not detached from history, but its final burden reaches beyond the ancient sanctuary crisis into the closing phase of the great controversy over worship, truth, and judgment.","Daniel's fear is understandable. To see centuries of conflict, persecution, and sanctuary issues in one vision is overwhelming. Yet Gabriel's command is gracious: understand. God does not give end-time truth to terrify His people, but to prepare them to trust Christ and stand faithfully.","The verse calls for sobriety. End-time prophecy is not a playground for speculation. It is light given by God so His people can discern false worship, honor the Prince, and rest in the sanctuary work of Christ."],"explanation":"Gabriel comes near, and Daniel is afraid and falls on his face. The picture is restless from the start. Wind, water, darkness, and conflict tell the reader that the kingdoms about to appear do not rise out of peace but out of the agitation of nations.","Scripture gives the symbols their meaning. Revelation 17:15 interprets waters as peoples, multitudes, nations, and tongues. Isaiah 17:12-13 compares nations to the rushing of many waters, and Isaiah 57:20 pictures the wicked as a troubled sea. Winds in prophetic language often mark scattering, strife, judgment, or restraint, as in Jeremiah 49:36-37 and Revelation 7:1-3.","The sea, then, is not a vague image for mystery. It is the world of peoples and nations out of which successive empires arise. Daniel is seeing world history from heaven's point of view. What looks impressive in royal inscriptions looks turbulent in prophecy, because power separated from God becomes unstable, violent, and proud.","The four winds are not random weather. They belong to the symbolic world of prophecy where political powers are shaken, restrained, released, and judged. Jeremiah 49:36 speaks of four winds scattering Elam. Revelation 7:1 pictures four angels holding back the winds so destructive forces do not break loose before God's servants are sealed. Daniel is seeing nations in motion under forces larger than diplomacy.","The great sea matters just as much. Revelation 17:15 gives the interpretive key by saying waters represent peoples, multitudes, nations, and tongues. That does not mean Daniel had Revelation in front of him; it means the Bible's prophetic language is consistent. Empires rise out of humanity's restless political sea, not from the calm of righteousness. Daniel's world is not controlled by chance currents; the sea is troubled, but God is already moving the vision toward His throne.","The four winds coming from heaven also keep the vision from becoming fatalistic. Nations move, armies rise, governments shake, and peoples are stirred, but the winds are not independent gods. Daniel sees turmoil under heaven's supervision. That is why the same chapter can describe violent waters and still move calmly to a throne. The storm is real, but it is not ultimate.","This symbol gives readers a biblical way to think about history. Political upheaval is not proof that God has lost control. It is often the very setting in which prophecy reveals His control most clearly. Daniel does not invite God's people to trust the sea, but to trust the God who can show what rises from it and where it ends."],"explanation":"Daniel sees the vision by night: the four winds of heaven strive upon the great sea. Daniel has already seen the sequence of kingdoms in chapter 2 as metals in an image; now he sees the same line of history through beasts. The change of symbol matters. Earth may admire the splendor of empire, but heaven exposes its predatory character.","The angel later explains that the beasts are kings or kingdoms. Daniel 7:17 says the four beasts are four kings, and Daniel 7:23 clarifies that the fourth beast is a kingdom. Daniel 8 confirms this method by naming the ram as Medo-Persia and the goat as Greece. The Bible itself interprets beasts as ruling powers.","The four beasts correspond to Babylon, Medo-Persia, Greece, and Rome. The prophetic dominance dates are Babylon 605-539 B.C., Medo-Persia 539-331 B.C., Greece 331-168 B.C., and Rome 168 B.C.-A.D. 476. After Rome's western fragmentation, divided Europe becomes the setting in which the little horn rises.","Daniel 2 and Daniel 7 should be read together. Daniel 2 shows the kingdoms as metals in a man-shaped image, the way empire likes to present itself: glorious, ordered, impressive, and valuable. Daniel 7 shows the same line as beasts, the way heaven unmasks power when it becomes proud, violent, and predatory. The two chapters are not competing outlines; they are two angles on the same history.","The sequence is also expanded later in Daniel. Daniel 8 names Medo-Persia and Greece directly, so the reader is not left guessing. Rome is identified by its place as the fourth power after Greece and by its iron-like crushing character matching Daniel 2. The chapter trains us to let Scripture build the interpretation step by step rather than forcing symbols to fit private imagination.","The order of the beasts also protects the reader from a purely symbolic reading that floats away from history. Daniel is not speaking only about evil in general. He is tracing a succession. Daniel 2 gives the metal image, Daniel 7 gives the beast image, Daniel 8 names two of the kingdoms, and Daniel 11 follows the later struggles in greater detail. Prophecy is moral, but it is also historical.","At the same time, the beast imagery keeps the dates from becoming dry. These kingdoms are not merely entries on a timeline. They reveal what human authority becomes when it is severed from worship, humility, and justice. The chapter teaches the mind to follow history and the conscience to distrust beastly power."],"explanation":"Four great beasts come up from the sea, each different from the others. Daniel 4 has already shown Nebuchadnezzar becoming beastlike through pride and being restored to reason when he lifts his eyes to heaven. Daniel 7 compresses that lesson into the lion: empire becomes beastly when it forgets God, and humanity is restored only under the rule of the Most High.","Babylon's lion-like majesty makes the later judgment more solemn. The kingdom is dual, but not equal. Daniel 8's ram has two horns, with one higher than the other and the higher coming up last. That matches the historical movement in which Persia became the dominant side of the Medo-Persian power. The bear raised on one side is a visual way of saying the same thing.","The three ribs should be handled as a broad conquest image rather than a point for needless speculation. Daniel is describing not only conquest but a system that grinds down resistance.","Rome's connection to Daniel 2 should remain firm. He confirms the covenant with many for one week, and in the midst of the week He causes sacrifice and oblation to cease. The central figure is Messiah, not a future antichrist.","The reasons are textual and theological. The nearest dominant person in the prophecy is Messiah. Verse 24 has already named reconciliation, everlasting righteousness, and sanctuary fulfillment as the goals of the seventy weeks. Verse 26 says Messiah is cut off. Verse 27 explains what His death accomplishes: He confirms the covenant and brings the sacrificial system to its fulfillment.","The covenant is confirmed through Christ's ministry and death. From A.D. 27 to A.D. 34, the covenant invitation is directed especially to Israel: first through Jesus' own ministry, then through the apostolic witness in Jerusalem. In the midst of the week, A.D. 31, Christ dies. His death does not immediately stop temple rituals outwardly, but it ends their saving meaning because the true Lamb has been offered.","This is why the veil of the temple is torn at Christ's death and Hebrews can say that animal sacrifices cannot perfect the conscience. Jesus does not abolish sacrifice by desecrating a temple; He fulfills sacrifice by offering Himself once for all. The sanctuary meaning of the prophecy is therefore centered in Christ's priestly and sacrificial work.","There is no textual need to insert a future seven-year tribulation gap between the sixty-ninth and seventieth weeks. The seventy weeks are presented as one continuous period determined for Daniel's people and holy city. The final week comes naturally after the sixty-nine. To remove it to the end of history shifts the prophecy away from Messiah just where Gabriel has placed Him at the center.","The prophecy does speak of desolation. Jerusalem's destruction by Rome and the continuing consequences of rejecting Messiah are real. But those desolations do not require turning the covenant-confirming 'he' into an end-time enemy or making the seventieth week a future tribulation. Daniel 9 is Christ-centered, covenant-centered, and cross-centered.","The text itself supports the no-gap reading:\n1. The seventy weeks are one determined period for Daniel's people and city.\n2. The seventieth week comes after the sixty-nine weeks without any stated break.\n3. Messiah is the central person in the immediate context.\n4. The covenant language fits Christ's ministry and death.\n5. The sacrifices cease because Christ fulfills them, not because an end-time enemy interrupts them.\n6. A future seven-year tribulation gap shifts the focus away from the cross just where Gabriel places it."],"explanation":"Verse 27 describes the final week.
